href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Programming_Language_for_Business" title="Programming Language for Business">PL/B</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" by* COBOL. (October 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></td>/n</tr>/n<tr>/n<td colspan="2" class="hlist" style="text-align:center;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; padding-top: 3px;">/n<ul>/n<li><a href="/wiki/File:Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/16px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png" width="16" height="16" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/24px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/32px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="400" /></a> <a href="https://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/COBOL" class="extiw" title="wikibooks:COBOL">COBOL</a> at Wikibooks</li>/n</ul>/n</td>/n</tr>/n</table>/n<p><b>COBOL</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="/ɒ/: 'o' in 'body'">ɒ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span></span>/</a></span></span>, an <a href="/wiki/Acronym" title="Acronym">acronym</a> for <i>common business-oriented language</i>) is a <a href="/wiki/Compiled" class="mw-redirect" title="Compiled">compiled</a> English-like <a href="/wiki/Computer_programming_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Computer programming language">computer programming language</a> designed for business use. It is <a href="/wiki/Imperative_programming" title="Imperative programming">imperative</a>, <a href="/wiki/Procedural_programming" title="Procedural programming">procedural</a> and, since 2002, <a href="/wiki/Object-oriented_programming" title="Object-oriented programming">object-oriented</a>. COBOL is primarily used in business, finance, and administrative systems for companies and governments. COBOL is still widely used in legacy applications deployed on <a href="/wiki/Mainframe_computer" title="Mainframe computer">mainframe computers</a>, such as large-scale <a href="/wiki/Batch_processing" title="Batch processing">batch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transaction_processing" title="Transaction processing">transaction processing</a> jobs. But due to its declining popularity and the retirement of experienced COBOL programmers, programs are being migrated to new platforms, rewritten in modern languages or replaced with software packages.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[6]</a></sup> Most programming in COBOL is now purely to maintain existing applications.<sup id="cite_ref-Computerworld_Not_Dead_Yet_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Computerworld_Not_Dead_Yet-8">[7]</a></sup></p>/n<p>COBOL was designed in 1959 by <a href="/wiki/CODASYL" title="CODASYL">CODASYL</a> and was partly based on previous programming language design work by <a href="/wiki/Grace_Hopper" title="Grace Hopper">Grace Hopper</a>, commonly referred to as "the (grand)mother of COBOL".<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup> It was created as part of a <a href="/wiki/US_Department_of_Defense" class="mw-redirect" title="US Department of Defense">US Department of Defense</a> effort to create a <a href="/wiki/Software_portability" title="Software portability">portable</a> programming language for data processing. Intended as a stopgap, the Department of Defense promptly forced computer manufacturers to provide it, resulting in its widespread adoption.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup> It was <a href="/wiki/Standardized" class="mw-redirect" title="Standardized">standardized</a> in 1968 and has since been revised four times. Expansions include support for <a href="/wiki/Structured_programming" title="Structured programming">structured</a> and <a href="/wiki/Object-oriented_programming" title="Object-oriented programming">object-oriented programming</a>. The current standard is <i><a href="/wiki/ISO" class="mw-redirect" title="ISO">ISO</a>/<a href="/wiki/International_Electrotechnical_Commission" title="International Electrotechnical Commission">IEC</a> 1989:2014</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup></p>/n<p>COBOL has an English-like syntax, which was designed to be self-documenting and highly readable. However, it is verbose and uses over 300 <a href="/wiki/Reserved_word" title="Reserved word">reserved words</a>. In contrast with modern, succinct syntax like <code class="mw-highlight" id="" style="" dir="ltr"><span class="n">y</span> <span class="o">=</span> <span class="n">x</span><span class="p">;</span></code>, COBOL has a more English-like syntax (in this case, <code class="mw-highlight" id="" style="" dir="ltr"><span class="kr">MOVE</span> <span class="nv">x</span> <span class="kp">TO</span> <span class="nv">y</span></code>). COBOL code is split into four <i>divisions</i> (identification, environment, data and procedure) containing a rigid hierarchy of sections, paragraphs and sentences. Lacking a large <a href="/wiki/Standard_library" title="Standard library">standard library</a>, the standard specifies 43 statements, 87 functions and just one class.</p>/n<p>Academic computer scientists were generally uninterested in business applications when COBOL was created and were not involved in its design; it was (effectively) designed from the ground up as a computer language for business, with an emphasis on inputs and outputs, whose only data types were numbers and strings of text.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup> COBOL has been criticized throughout its life, however, for its verbosity, design process and poor support for <a href="/wiki/Structured_programming" title="Structured programming">structured programming</a>, which resulted in monolithic and incomprehensible programs.</p>/n<p></p>/n<div id="toc" class="toc">/n<div class="toctitle">/n<h2>Contents</h2>/n</div>/n<ul>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a class="tocnumber">2.5</span> <span class="toctext">Data division</span></a>/n<ul>/n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-20"><a href="#Aggregated_data"><span class="tocnumber">2.5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Aggregated data</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-21"><a href="#Other_data_levels"><span class="tocnumber">2.5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Other data levels</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-22"><a href="#Data_types"><span class="tocnumber">2.5.3</span> <span class="toctext">Data types</span></a>/n<ul>/n<li class="toclevel-4 tocsection-23"><a href="#PICTURE_clause"><span class="tocnumber">2.5.3.1</span> <span class="toctext">PICTURE clause</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-4 tocsection-24"><a href="#USAGE_clause"><span class="tocnumber">2.5.3.2</span> <span class="toctext">USAGE clause</span></a></li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-25"><a href="#Report_writer"><span class="tocnumber">2.5.4</span> <span class="toctext">Report writer</span></a></li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-26"><a href="#Procedure_division"><span class="tocnumber">2.6</span> <span class="toctext">Procedure division</span></a>/n<ul>/n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-27"><a href="#Procedures"><span class="tocnumber">2.6.1</span> <span class="toctext">Procedures</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-28"><a href="#Statements"><span class="tocnumber">2.6.2</span> <span class="toctext">Statements</span></a>/n<ul>/n<li class="toclevel-4 tocsection-29"><a href="#Control_flow"><span class="tocnumber">2.6.2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Control flow</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-4 tocsection-30"><a href="#I.2FO"><span class="tocnumber">2.6.2.2</span> <span class="toctext">I/O</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-4 tocsection-31"><a href="#Data_manipulation"><span class="tocnumber">2.6.2.3</span> <span class="toctext">Data manipulation</span></a></li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-32"><a href="#Scope_termination"><span class="tocnumber">2.6.3</span> <span class="toctext">Scope termination</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-33"><a href="#Self-modifying_code"><span class="tocnumber">2.6.4</span> <span class="toctext">Self-modifying code</span></a></li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-34"><a href="#Hello.2C_world"><span class="tocnumber">2.7</span> <span class="toctext">Hello, world</span></a></li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-35"><a href="#Criticism_and_defense"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Criticism and defense</span></a>/n<ul>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-36"><a href="#Lack_of_structure"><span class="tocnumber">3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Lack of structure</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-37"><a href="#Compatibility_issues"><span class="tocnumber">3.2</span> <span class="toctext">Compatibility issues</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-38"><a href="#Verbose_syntax"><span class="tocnumber">3.3</span> <span class="toctext">Verbose syntax</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-39"><a href="#Isolation_from_the_computer_science_community"><span class="tocnumber">3.4</span> <span class="toctext">Isolation from the computer science community</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-40"><a href="#Concerns_about_the_design_process"><span class="tocnumber">3.5</span> <span class="toctext">Concerns about the design process</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-41"><a href="#Influences_on_other_languages"><span class="tocnumber">3.6</span> <span class="toctext">Influences on other languages</span></a></li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-42"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-43"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-44"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a>/n<ul>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-45"><a href="#Citations"><span class="tocnumber">6.1</span> <span class="toctext">Citations</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-46"><a href="#Sources"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext">Sources</span></a></li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-47"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>/n</ul>/n</div>/n<p></p>/n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_and_specification">History and specification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=COBOL&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History and specification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=COBOL&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<p>In the late 1950s, computer users and manufacturers were becoming concerned about the rising cost of programming. A 1959 survey had found that in any data processing installation, the programming cost US$800,000 on average and that translating programs to run on new hardware would cost $600,000. At a time when new <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_programming_languages" title="Timeline of programming languages">programming languages were proliferating</a> at an ever-increasing rate, the same survey suggested that if a common business-oriented language were used, conversion would be far cheaper and faster.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeyer2009282_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeyer2009282-15">[14]</a></sup></p>/n<div class="thumb tright">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Grace_Hopper.jpg" class="image"><img alt="caption" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Grace_Hopper.jpg/220px-Grace_Hopper.jpg" width="220" height="258" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Grace_Hopper.jpg/330px-Grace_Hopper.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Grace_Hopper.jpg/440px-Grace_Hopper.jpg 2x" data-file-width="517" data-file-height="606" /></a>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Grace_Hopper.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>/n<a href="/wiki/Grace_Hopper" title="Grace Hopper">Grace Hopper</a>, the inventor of <a href="/wiki/FLOW-MATIC" title="FLOW-MATIC">FLOW-MATIC</a>, a predecessor to COBOL</div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<p>In April 1959, <a href="/wiki/Mary_K._Hawes" title="Mary K. Hawes">Mary K. Hawes</a> called a meeting of representatives from academia, computer users, and manufacturers at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pennsylvania" title="University of Pennsylvania">University of Pennsylvania</a> to organize a formal meeting on common business languages.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup> Representatives included <a href="/wiki/Grace_Hopper" title="Grace Hopper">Grace Hopper</a>, inventor of the English-like data processing language <a href="/wiki/FLOW-MATIC" title="FLOW-MATIC">FLOW-MATIC</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean_Sammet" class="mw-redirect" title="Jean Sammet">Jean Sammet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saul_Gorn" title="Saul Gorn">Saul Gorn</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeyer2009281.E2.80.93282_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeyer2009281.E2.80.93282-17">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESammet1978a200_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESammet1978a200-18">[17]</a></sup></p>/n<p>The group asked the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense" title="United States Department of Defense">Department of Defense</a> (DoD) to sponsor an effort to create a common business language. The delegation impressed Charles A. Phillips, director of the Data System Research Staff at the DoD, who thought that they "thoroughly understood" the DoD's problems. The DoD operated 225 computers, had a further 175 on order and had spent over $200 million on implementing programs to run on them. Portable programs would save time, reduce costs and ease modernization.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeyer2009283_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeyer2009283-19">[18]</a></sup></p>/n<p>Phillips agreed to sponsor the meeting and tasked the delegation with drafting the agenda.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeyer2009284_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeyer2009284-20">[19]</a></sup></p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="COBOL_60">COBOL 60</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=COBOL&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: COBOL 60">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<p>On May 28 and 29 of 1959 (exactly one year after the Zürich <a href="/wiki/ALGOL_58" title="ALGOL 58">ALGOL 58</a> meeting), a meeting was held at the Pentagon to discuss the creation of a common programming language for business. It was attended by 41 people and was chaired by Phillips.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup> The Department of Defense was concerned about whether it could run the same data processing programs on different computers. <a href="/wiki/FORTRAN" class="mw-redirect" title="FORTRAN">FORTRAN</a>, the only mainstream language at the time, lacked the features needed to write such programs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESammet2004104_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESammet2004104-22">[21]</a></sup></p>/n<p>Representatives enthusiastically described a language that could work in a wide variety of environments, from banking and insurance to utilities and inventory control. They agreed unanimously that more people should be able to program and that the new language should not be restricted by the limitations of contemporary technology. A majority agreed that the language should make maximal use of English, be capable of change, be machine-independent and be easy to use, even at the expense of power.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeyer2009286_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeyer2009286-23">[22]</a></sup></p>/n<p>The meeting resulted in the creation of a <a href="/wiki/Steering_committee" class="mw-redirect" title="Steering committee">steering committee</a> and short-, intermediate- and long-range committees. The short-range committee was given to September (three months) to produce specifications for an interim language, which would then be improved upon by the other committees.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConner1984ID.2F9_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConner1984ID.2F9-24">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESammet1978a201_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESammet1978a201-25">[24]</a></sup> Their official mission, however, was to identify the strengths and weaknesses of existing programming languages and did not explicitly direct them to create a new language.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESammet2004104_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESammet2004104-22">[21]</a></sup> The deadline was met with disbelief by the short-range committee.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBemer1971132_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBemer1971132-26">[25]</a></sup> One member, <a href="/wiki/Betty_Holberton" title="Betty Holberton">Betty Holberton</a>, described the three-month deadline as "gross optimism" and doubted that the language really would be a stopgap.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeyer2009288_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeyer2009288-27">[26]</a></sup></p>/n<p>The steering committee met on June 4 and agreed to name the entire activity as the <i>Committee on Data Systems Languages</i>, or <a href="/wiki/CODASYL" title="CODASYL">CODASYL</a>, and to form an executive committee.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESammet1978a203_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESammet1978a203-28">[27]</a></sup></p>/n<p>The short-range committee was made up of members representing six computer manufacturers and three government agencies. The six computer manufacturers were <a href="/wiki/Burroughs_Corporation" title="Burroughs Corporation">Burroughs Corporation</a>, <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a>, <a href="/wiki/Honeywell" title="Honeywell">Minneapolis-Honeywell</a> (Honeywell Labs), <a href="/wiki/RCA" title="RCA">RCA</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sperry_Rand" class="mw-redirect" title="Sperry Rand">Sperry Rand</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sylvania_Electric_Products" title="Sylvania Electric Products">Sylvania Electric Products</a>. The three government agencies were the <a href="/wiki/US_Air_Force" class="mw-redirect" title="US Air Force">US Air Force</a>, the Navy's <a href="/wiki/David_Taylor_Model_Basin" title="David Taylor Model Basin">David Taylor Model Basin</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/National_Bureau_of_Standards" class="mw-redirect" title="National Bureau of Standards">National Bureau of Standards</a> (now the National Institute of Standards and Technology).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECODASYL1969.C2.A7_I.2.1.1_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECODASYL1969.C2.A7_I.2.1.1-29">[28]</a></sup> The committee was chaired by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Wegstein&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Joseph Wegstein (page does not exist)">Joseph Wegstein</a> of the US National Bureau of Standards. Work began by investigating data description, statements, existing applications and user experiences.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESammet1978a204_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESammet1978a204-30">[29]</a></sup></p>/n<p>The committee mainly examined the <a href="/wiki/FLOW-MATIC" title="FLOW-MATIC">FLOW-MATIC</a>, <a href="/wiki/AIMACO" title="AIMACO">AIMACO</a> and <a href="/wiki/COMTRAN" title="COMTRAN">COMTRAN</a> programming languages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESammet2004104_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESammet2004104-22">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECODASYL1969.C2.A7_I.1.2_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECODASYL1969.C2.A7_I.1.2-31">[30]</a></sup> The FLOW-MATIC language was particularly influential because it had been implemented and because AIMACO was a derivative of it with only minor changes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeyer2009290_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeyer2009290-32">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-cisn_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cisn-33">[32]</a></sup> FLOW-MATIC's inventor, Grace Hopper, also served as a technical adviser to the committee.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBemer1971132_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBemer1971132-26">[25]</a></sup> FLOW-MATIC's major contributions to COBOL were long variable names, English words for commands and the separation of data descriptions and instructions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESammet1978a217_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESammet1978a217-34">[33]</a></sup></p>/n<p>IBM's COMTRAN language, invented by <a href="/wiki/Bob_Bemer" title="Bob Bemer">Bob Bemer</a>, was regarded as a competitor to FLOW-MATIC<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeyer2009292_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeyer2009292-35">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBemer1971131_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBemer1971131-36">[35]</a></sup> by a short-range committee made up of colleagues of Grace Hopper.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeyer2009296_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeyer2009296-37">[36]</a></sup> Some of its features were not incorporated into COBOL so that it would not look like IBM had dominated the design process,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConner1984ID.2F9_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConner1984ID.2F9-24">[23]</a></sup> and Jean Sammet said in 1981 that there had been a "strong anti-IBM bias" from some committee members (herself included).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESammet1978a221_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESammet1978a221-38">[37]</a></sup> In one case, after Roy Goldfinger, author of the COMTRAN manual and intermediate-range committee member, attended a subcommittee meeting to support his language and encourage the use of algebraic expressions, Grace Hopper sent a memo to the short-range committee reiterating Sperry Rand's efforts to create a language based on English.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeyer2009291_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeyer2009291-39">[38]</a></sup> In 1980, Grace Hopper commented that "COBOL 60 is 95% FLOW-MATIC" and that COMTRAN had had an "extremely small" influence. Furthermore, she said that she would claim that work was influenced by both FLOW-MATIC and COMTRAN only to "keep other people happy [so they] wouldn't try to knock us out".<sup id="cite_ref-Hopper_Oral_History_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hopper_Oral_History-40">[39]</a></sup> Features from COMTRAN incorporated into COBOL included formulas,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESammet1978a218_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESammet1978a218-41">[40]</a></sup> the <a href="#PICTURE_clause"><code class="mw-highlight" id="" style="" dir="ltr">PICTURE</code> clause</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarcotty1978268_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarcotty1978268-42">[41]</a></sup> an improved <code>IF</code> statement, which obviated the need for <a href="/wiki/GO_TO" class="mw-redirect" title="GO TO">GO TOs</a>, and a more robust file management system.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeyer2009292_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeyer2009292-35">[34]</a></sup></p>/n<p>The usefulness of the committee's work was subject of great debate. While some members thought the language had too many compromises and was the result of <a href="/wiki/Design_by_committee" title="Design by committee">design by committee</a>, others felt it was better than the three languages examined. Some felt the language was too complex; others, too simple.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESammet1978a205.E2.80.93206_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESammet1978a205.E2.80.93206-43">[42]</a></sup> Controversial features included those some considered useless or too advanced for data processing users. Such features included <a href="/wiki/Boolean_expression" title="Boolean expression">boolean expressions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Formula" title="Formula">formulas</a> and table <i><dfn>subscripts</dfn></i> (indices).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESammet1978aFigure_8_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESammet1978aFigure_8-44">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESammet1978a230.E2.80.93231_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESammet1978a230.E2.80.93231-45">[44]</a></sup> Another point of controversy was whether to make keywords context-sensitive and the effect that would have on readability.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESammet1978aFigure_8_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESammet1978aFigure_8-44">[43]</a></sup> Although context-sensitive keywords were rejected, the approach was later used in <a href="/wiki/PL/I" title="PL/I">PL/I</a> and partially in COBOL from 2002.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEISO.2FIEC_JTC_1.2FSC_22.2FWG_42001846_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEISO.2FIEC_JTC_1.2FSC_22.2FWG_42001846-46">[45]</a></sup> Little consideration was given to <a href="/wiki/Interactivity" title="Interactivity">interactivity</a>, interaction with <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">operating systems</a> (few existed at that time) and functions (thought of as purely mathematical and of no use in data processing).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESammet1978a220_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESammet1978a220-47">[46]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESammet1978a228_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESammet1978a228-48">[47]</a></sup></p>/n<p>The specifications were presented to the Executive Committee on September 4. They fell short of expectations: Joseph Wegstein noted that "it contains rough spots and requires some additions", and Bob Bemer later described them as a "hodgepodge". The subcommittee was given until December to improve it.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBemer1971132_26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBemer1971132-26">[25]</a></sup></p>/n<p>At a mid-September meeting, the committee discussed the new language's name. Suggestions included "BUSY" (Business System), "INFOSYL" (Information System Language) and "COCOSYL" (Common Computer Systems Language).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESammet1978a210_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESammet1978a210-49">[48]</a></sup> The name "COBOL" was suggested by Bob Bemer.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup></p>/n<p>In October, the intermediate-range committee received copies of the <a href="/wiki/FACT_(computer_language)" title="FACT (computer language)">FACT</a> language specification created by <a href="/wiki/Roy_Nutt" title="Roy Nutt">Roy Nutt</a>. Its features impressed the committee so much that they passed a resolution to base COBOL on it.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeyer2009293_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeyer2009293-52">[51]</a></sup> This was a blow to the short-range committee, who had made good progress on the specification. Despite being technically superior, FACT had not been created with portability in mind or through manufacturer and user consensus. It also lacked a demonstrable implementation,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBemer1971132_26-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBemer1971132-26">[25]</a></sup> allowing supporters of a FLOW-MATIC-based COBOL to overturn the resolution. RCA representative Howard Bromberg also blocked FACT, so that RCA's work on a COBOL implementation would not go to waste.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeyer2009294_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeyer2009294-53">[52]</a></sup></p>/n<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; padding: 10px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">/n<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">/n<div style="margin:0px 2em;">/n<div class="poem">/n<p>'And what name do you want inscribed?'<br />/nI said, 'I'll write it for you.' I wrote the name down: COBOL.<br />/n'What kind of name is that?'<br />/n'Well it's a Polish name. We shortened it and got rid of a lot of unnecessary notation.'</p>/n</div>/n</div>/n<div style="position: absolute; left: 0px; top: 0px; text-align: left; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; font-size: large; color: gray;">“</div>/n<div style="position: absolute; right: 0px; bottom: 0px; text-align: left; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; font-size: large; color: gray;">”</div>/n</div>/n<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">Howard Bromberg on how he bought the COBOL tombstone<sup id="cite_ref-Tombstone_story_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tombstone_story-54">[53]</a></sup></cite></div>/n<p>It soon became apparent that the committee was too large for any further progress to be made quickly. A frustrated Howard Bromberg bought a $15 tombstone with "COBOL" engraved on it and sent it to Charles Phillips to demonstrate his displeasure.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[b]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tombstone_story_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tombstone_story-54">[53]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBemer1971130_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBemer1971130-57">[55]</a></sup> A sub-committee was formed to analyze existing languages and was made up of six individuals:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESammet2004104_22-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESammet2004104-22">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeyer2009289_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeyer2009289-58">[56]</a></sup></p>/n<ul>/n<li>William Selden and Gertrude Tierney of IBM,</li>/n<li>Howard Bromberg and Howard Discount of RCA,</li>/n<li>Vernon Reeves and <a href="/wiki/Jean_E._Sammet" title="Jean E. Sammet">Jean E. Sammet</a> of Sylvania Electric Products.</li>/n</ul>/n<p>The sub-committee did most of the work creating the specification, leaving the short-range committee to review and modify their work before producing the finished specification.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESammet2004104_22-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESammet2004104-22">[21]</a></sup></p>/n<div class="thumb tright">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:COBOL_Report_Apr60.djvu" class="image"><img alt="COBOL. Report to Conference on Data Systems Languages including initial specifications for a Common Business Oriented Language (COBOL) for programming digital electronic computers. Department of Defense, April 1960." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/COBOL_Report_Apr60.djvu/page1-220px-COBOL_Report_Apr60.djvu.jpg" width="220" height="289" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/COBOL_Report_Apr60.djvu/page1-330px-COBOL_Report_Apr60.djvu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/COBOL_Report_Apr60.djvu/page1-440px-COBOL_Report_Apr60.djvu.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2368" data-file-height="3108" /></a>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:COBOL_Report_Apr60.djvu" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>/nThe cover of the <i>COBOL 60</i> report</div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<p>The specifications were approved by the Executive Committee on January 3, 1960, and sent to the government printing office, which printed these as <i>COBOL 60</i>. The language's stated objectives were to allow efficient, portable programs to be easily written, to allow users to move to new systems with minimal effort and cost, and to be suitable for inexperienced programmers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECODASYL1969.C2.A7_I.1.1_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECODASYL1969.C2.A7_I.1.1-59">[57]</a></sup> The CODASYL Executive Committee later created the COBOL Maintenance Committee to answer questions from users and vendors and to improve and expand the specifications.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown197647_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown197647-60">[58]</a></sup></p>/n<p>During 1960, the list of manufacturers planning to build COBOL compilers grew. By September, five more manufacturers had joined CODASYL (<a href="/wiki/Bendix_Corporation" title="Bendix Corporation">Bendix</a>, <a href="/wiki/Control_Data_Corporation" title="Control Data Corporation">Control Data Corporation</a>, <a href="/wiki/General_Electric" title="General Electric">General Electric</a> (GE), <a href="/wiki/National_Cash_Register" class="mw-redirect" title="National Cash Register">National Cash Register</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philco" title="Philco">Philco</a>), and all represented manufacturers had announced COBOL compilers. GE and IBM planned to integrate COBOL into their own languages, GECOM and COMTRAN, respectively. In contrast, <a href="/wiki/International_Computers_and_Tabulators" title="International Computers and Tabulators">International Computers and Tabulators</a> planned to replace their language, CODEL, with COBOL.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBemer1971133_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBemer1971133-61">[59]</a></sup></p>/n<p>Meanwhile, RCA and Sperry Rand worked on creating COBOL compilers. The first COBOL program ran on 17 August on an RCA 501.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeyer2009297_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeyer2009297-62">[60]</a></sup> On December 6 and 7, the same COBOL program (albeit with minor changes) ran on an RCA computer and a Remington-Rand <a href="/wiki/Univac" class="mw-redirect" title="Univac">Univac</a> computer, demonstrating that compatibility could be achieved.<sup id="cite_ref-Williams12_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Williams12-63">[61]</a></sup></p>/n<p>The relative influences of which languages were used continues to this day in the recommended advisory printed in all COBOL reference manuals:</p>/n<blockquote class="templatequote">/n<p>COBOL is an industry language and is not the property of any company or group of companies, or of any organization or group of organizations.</p>/n<p>No warranty, expressed or implied, is made by any contributor or by the CODASYL COBOL Committee as to the accuracy and functioning of the programming system and language. Moreover, no responsibility is assumed by any contributor, or by the committee, in connection therewith. The authors and copyright holders of the copyrighted material used herein are as follows:</p>/n<dl>/n<dd>FLOW-MATIC (trademark of Unisys Corporation), Programming for the UNIVAC (R) I and II, Data Automation Systems, copyrighted 1958, 1959, by Unisys Corporation; IBM Commercial Translator Form No. F28-8013, copyrighted 1959 by IBM; FACT, DSI 27A5260-2760, copyrighted 1960 by Minneapolis-Honeywell.</dd>/n</dl>/n<p>They have specifically authorized the use of this material, in whole or in part, in the COBOL specifications. Such authorization extends to the reproduction and use of COBOL specifications in programming manuals or similar publications.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[62]</a></sup></p>/n</blockquote>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="COBOL-61_to_COBOL-65">COBOL-61 to COBOL-65</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=COBOL&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: COBOL-61 to COBOL-65">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:30%; padding: 10px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">/n<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">/n<div>It is rather unlikely that Cobol will be around by the end of the decade.</div>/n</div>/n<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">Anonymous, June 1960<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[63]</a></sup></cite></div>/n<p>Many logical flaws were found in <i>COBOL 60</i>, leading GE's <a href="/wiki/Charles_Katz" title="Charles Katz">Charles Katz</a> to warn that it could not be interpreted unambiguously. A reluctant short-term committee enacted a total cleanup and, by March 1963, it was reported that COBOL's syntax was as definable as <a href="/wiki/ALGOL" title="ALGOL">ALGOL</a>'s, although semantic ambiguities remained.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBemer1971133_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBemer1971133-61">[59]</a></sup></p>/n<p>Early COBOL compilers were primitive and slow. A 1962 US Navy evaluation found compilation speeds of 3–11 statements per minute. By mid-1964, they had increased to 11–1000 statements per minute. It was observed that increasing memory would drastically increase speed and that compilation costs varied wildly: costs per statement were between $0.23 and $18.91.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBemer1971134_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBemer1971134-66">[64]</a></sup></p>/n<p>In late 1962, IBM announced that COBOL would be their primary development language and that development of COMTRAN would cease.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBemer1971134_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBemer1971134-66">[64]</a></sup></p>/n<p>The COBOL specification was revised three times in the five years after its publication. COBOL-60 was replaced in 1961 by COBOL-61. This was then replaced by the COBOL-61 Extended specifications in 1963, which introduced the sort and report writer facilities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown197648_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown197648-67">[65]</a></sup> The added facilities corrected flaws identified by Honeywell in late 1959 in a letter to the short-range committee.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeyer2009297_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeyer2009297-62">[60]</a></sup> COBOL Edition 1965 brought further clarifications to the specifications and introduced facilities for handling <a href="/wiki/Mass_storage" title="Mass storage">mass storage</a> files and <a href="/wiki/Table_(information)" title="Table (information)">tables</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECODASYL1969.C2.A7_I.2.2.4_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECODASYL1969.C2.A7_I.2.2.4-68">[66]</a></sup></p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="COBOL-68">COBOL-68</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=COBOL&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: COBOL-68">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<p>Efforts began to standardize COBOL to overcome incompatibilities between versions. In late 1962, both ISO and the United States of America Standards Institute (now <a href="/wiki/ANSI" class="mw-redirect" title="ANSI">ANSI</a>) formed groups to create standards. ANSI produced <i>USA Standard COBOL X3.23</i> in August 1968, which became the cornerstone for later versions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECODASYL1969.C2.A7_I.2.3_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECODASYL1969.C2.A7_I.2.3-69">[67]</a></sup> This version was known as American National Standard (ANS) COBOL and was adopted by ISO in 1972.<sup id="cite_ref-FS2003_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FS2003-70">[68]</a></sup></p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="COBOL-74">COBOL-74</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=COBOL&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: COBOL-74">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<p>By 1970, COBOL had become the most widely used programming language in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeyer2009301_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeyer2009301-71">[69]</a></sup></p>/n<p>Independently of the ANSI committee, the CODASYL Programming Language Committee was working on improving the language. They described new versions in 1968, 1969, 1970 and 1973, including changes such as new inter-program communication, debugging and file merging facilities as well as improved string-handling and <a href="/wiki/Library_(computing)" title="Library (computing)">library</a> inclusion features.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown197649_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown197649-72">[70]</a></sup> Although CODASYL was independent of the ANSI committee, the <i>CODASYL Journal of Development</i> was used by ANSI to identify features that were popular enough to warrant implementing.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown197652_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown197652-73">[71]</a></sup> The Programming Language Committee also liaised with <a href="/wiki/Ecma_International" title="Ecma International">ECMA</a> and the Japanese COBOL Standard committee.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrown197649_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrown197649-72">[70]</a></sup></p>/n<p>The Programming Language Committee was not well-known, however. The vice-president, William Rinehuls, complained that two-thirds of the COBOL community did not know of the committee's existence. It was also poor, lacking the funds to make public documents, such as minutes of meetings and change proposals, freely available.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[72]</a></sup></p>/n<p>In 1974, ANSI published a revised version of (ANS) COBOL, containing new features such as <a href="/wiki/COBOL#Files" title="COBOL">file organizations</a>, the <code class="mw-highlight" id="" style="" dir="ltr">DELETE</code> statement<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[73]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Memory_segmentation" title="Memory segmentation">segmentation</a> module.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKlein201016_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlein201016-76">[74]</a></sup> Deleted features included the <code class="mw-highlight" id="" style="" dir="ltr">NOTE</code> statement, the <code class="mw-highlight" id="" style="" dir="ltr">EXAMINE</code> statement (which was replaced by <code class="mw-highlight" id="" style="" dir="ltr">INSPECT</code>) and the implementer-defined random access module (which was superseded by the new sequential and relative I/O modules). These made up 44 changes, which rendered existing statements incompatible with the new standard.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[75]</a></sup> The report writer was slated to be removed from COBOL, but was reinstated before the standard was published.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[77]</a></sup> ISO later adopted the updated standard in 1978.<sup id="cite_ref-FS2003_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FS2003-70">[68]</a></sup></p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="COBOL-85">COBOL-85</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=COBOL&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: COBOL-85">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<p>In June 1978, work began on revising COBOL-74. The proposed standard (commonly called COBOL-80) differed significantly from the previous one, causing concerns about incompatibility and conversion costs. In January 1981, Joseph T. Brophy, Senior Vice-President of Travelers Insurance, threatened to sue the standard committee because it was not <a href="/wiki/Forward_compatibility" title="Forward compatibility">upwards compatible</a> with COBOL-74. Mr. Brophy described previous conversions of their 40-million-line code base as "non-productive" and a "complete waste of our programmer resources".<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[78]</a></sup> Later that year, the <a href="/wiki/Data_Processing_Management_Association" class="mw-redirect" title="Data Processing Management Association">Data Processing Management Association</a> (DPMA) said it was "strongly opposed" to the new standard, citing "prohibitive" conversion costs and enhancements that were "forced on the user".<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[79]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Computerworld_19_37_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Computerworld_19_37-82">[80]</a></sup></p>/n<p>During the first public review period, the committee received 2,200 responses, of which 1,700 were negative form letters.<sup id="cite_ref-Computerworld_Garfunkel_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Computerworld_Garfunkel-83">[81]</a></sup> Other responses were detailed analyses of the effect COBOL-80 would have on their systems; conversion costs were predicted to be at least 50 cents per line of code. Fewer than a dozen of the responses were in favor of the proposed standard.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[82]</a></sup></p>/n<p>In 1983, the DPMA withdrew its opposition to the standard, citing the responsiveness of the committee to public concerns. In the same year, a National Bureau of Standards study concluded that the proposed standard would present few problems.<sup id="cite_ref-Computerworld_19_37_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Computerworld_19_37-82">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[83]</a></sup> A year later, a COBOL-80 compiler was released to <a href="/wiki/DEC_VAX" class="mw-redirect" title="DEC VAX">DEC VAX</a> users, who noted that conversion of COBOL-74 programs posed few problems. The new <code>EVALUATE</code> statement and inline <code>PERFORM</code> were particularly well received and improved productivity, thanks to simplified <a href="/wiki/Control_flow" title="Control flow">control flow</a> and <a href="/wiki/Debugging" title="Debugging">debugging</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[84]</a></sup></p>/n<p>The second public review drew another 1,000 (mainly negative) responses, while the last drew just 25, by which time many concerns had been addressed.<sup id="cite_ref-Computerworld_19_37_82-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Computerworld_19_37-82">[80]</a></sup></p>/n<p>In late 1985, ANSI published the revised standard. Sixty features were changed or deprecated and many<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="This term requires quantification. (March 2014)">quantify</span></a></i>]</sup> were added, such as:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGarfunkel1987150_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGarfunkel1987150-87">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[86]</a></sup></p>/n<ul>/n<li>Scope terminators (<code>END-IF</code>, <code>END-PERFORM</code>, <code>END-READ</code>, etc.)</li>/n<li>Nested subprograms</li>/n<li><code>CONTINUE</code>, a <a href="/wiki/Placeholder_(Computer_syntax)" class="mw-redirect" title="Placeholder (Computer syntax)">no-operation statement</a></li>/n<li><code>EVALUATE</code>, a <a href="/wiki/Switch_statement" title="Switch statement">switch statement</a></li>/n<li><code>INITIALIZE</code>, a statement that can set groups of data to their default values</li>/n<li>Inline <code>PERFORM</code> loop bodies – previously, loop bodies had to be specified in a separate procedure</li>/n<li>Reference modification, which allows access to substrings</li>/n<li>I/O status codes.</li>/n</ul>/n<p>The standard was adopted by ISO the same year.<sup id="cite_ref-FS2003_70-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FS2003-70">[68]</a></sup> Two amendments followed in 1989 and 1993, the first introducing intrinsic functions and the other providing corrections. ISO adopted the amendments in 1991 and 1994 respectively,<sup id="cite_ref-FS2003_70-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FS2003-70">[68]</a></sup> before subsequently taking primary ownership and development of the standard.</p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="COBOL_2002_and_object-oriented_COBOL">COBOL 2002 and object-oriented COBOL</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=COBOL&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: COBOL 2002 and object-oriented COBOL">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<p>In 1997, <a href="/wiki/Gartner_Group" class="mw-redirect" title="Gartner Group">Gartner Group</a> estimated that there were a total of 200 billion lines of COBOL in existence, which ran 80% of all business programs.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[87]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="While this claim is ubiquitous, the actual Gartner report is needed to verify the claim. (January 2015)">better&#160;source&#160;needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>/n<p>In the early 1990s, work began on adding object-orientation in the next full revision of COBOL. Object-oriented features were taken from <a href="/wiki/C%2B%2B" title="C++">C++</a> and <a href="/wiki/Smalltalk" title="Smalltalk">Smalltalk</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-SW95_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SW95-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Arranga98_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arranga98-2">[2]</a></sup> The initial estimate was to have this revision completed by 1997, and an ISO Committee Draft (CD) was available by 1997. Some vendors (including <a href="/wiki/Micro_Focus_International" class="mw-redirect" title="Micro Focus International">Micro Focus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fujitsu" title="Fujitsu">Fujitsu</a>, and <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a>) introduced object-oriented syntax based on drafts of the full revision. The final approved ISO standard was approved and published in late 2002.<sup id="cite_ref-cobolstandards.com_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cobolstandards.com-90">[88]</a></sup></p>/n<p>Fujitsu/GTSoftware,<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[89]</a></sup> Micro Focus and <a href="/w/index.php?title=RainCode&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="RainCode (page does not exist)">RainCode</a> introduced object-oriented COBOL compilers targeting the <a href="/wiki/.NET_Framework" title=".NET Framework">.NET Framework</a>.</p>/n<p>There were many other new features, many of which had been in the <i>CODASYL COBOL Journal of Development</i> since 1978 and had missed the opportunity to be included in COBOL-85.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[90]</a></sup> These other features included:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEISO.2FIEC_JTC_1.2FSC_22.2FWG_42001Annex_F_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEISO.2FIEC_JTC_1.2FSC_22.2FWG_42001Annex_F-93">[91]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKlein201021_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlein201021-94">[92]</a></sup></p>/n<ul>/n<li>Free-form code</li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/User-defined_function" title="User-defined function">User-defined functions</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Recursion" title="Recursion">Recursion</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Locale_(computer_software)" title="Locale (computer software)">Locale</a>-based processing</li>/n<li>Support for extended character sets such as <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Floating-point" class="mw-redirect" title="Floating-point">Floating-point</a> and <a href="/wiki/Binary_number" title="Binary number">binary</a> data types (until then, binary items were truncated based on their declaration's <a href="/wiki/Base-10" class="mw-redirect" title="Base-10">base-10</a> specification)</li>/n<li>Portable arithmetic results</li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Bit" title="Bit">Bit</a> and <a href="/wiki/Boolean_data_type" title="Boolean data type">boolean</a> data types</li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Pointer_(computer_programming)" title="Pointer (computer programming)">Pointers</a> and syntax for getting and freeing storage</li>/n<li>The <code class="mw-highlight" id="" style="" dir="ltr">SCREEN SECTION</code> for <a href="/wiki/Text-based_user_interface" title="Text-based user interface">text-based user interfaces</a></li>/n<li>The <code class="mw-highlight" id="" style="" dir="ltr">VALIDATE</code> facility</li>/n<li>Improved interoperability with other programming languages and <a href="/wiki/Application_framework" title="Application framework">framework environments</a> such as .NET and <a href="/wiki/Java_(platform)" class="mw-redirect" title="Java (platform)">Java</a>.</li>/n</ul>/n<p>Three <a href="/wiki/Erratum" title="Erratum">corrigenda</a> were published for the standard: two in 2006 and one in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-WG4_Website_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WG4_Website-95">[93]</a></sup></p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="COBOL_2014">COBOL 2014</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=COBOL&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: COBOL 2014">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<p>Between 2003 and 2009, three technical reports were produced describing <a href="/wiki/Finalizer" title="Finalizer">object finalization</a>, <a href="/wiki/XML" title="XML">XML</a> processing and <a href="/wiki/Collection_class" class="mw-redirect" title="Collection class">collection classes</a> for COBOL.<sup id="cite_ref-WG4_Website_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WG4_Website-95">[93]</a></sup></p>/n<p>COBOL 2002 suffered from poor support: no compilers completely supported the standard. Micro Focus found that it was due to a lack of user demand for the new features and due to the abolition of the <a href="/wiki/NIST" class="mw-redirect" title="NIST">NIST</a> test suite, which had been used to test compiler conformance. The standardization process was also found to be slow and under-resourced.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[94]</a></sup></p>/n<p>COBOL 2014 includes the following changes:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEISO.2FIEC_JTC_1.2FSC_22.2FWG_42014Annex_E_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEISO.2FIEC_JTC_1.2FSC_22.2FWG_42014Annex_E-97">[95]</a></sup></p>/n<ul>/n<li>Portable arithmetic results have been replaced by <a href="/wiki/IEEE_754" title="IEEE 754">IEEE 754</a> data types</li>/n<li>Major features have been made optional, such as the <code>VALIDATE</code> facility, the report writer and the screen-handling facility.</li>/n<li>Method overloading</li>/n<li>Dynamic capacity tables (a feature dropped from the draft of COBOL 2002)<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[96]</a></sup></li>/n</ul>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=COBOL&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<p>COBOL programs are used globally in governments and businesses and are running on diverse operating systems such as <a href="/wiki/Z/OS" title="Z/OS">z/OS</a>, <a href="/wiki/Z/VSE" class="mw-redirect" title="Z/VSE">z/VSE</a>, <a href="/wiki/ICL_VME" title="ICL VME">VME</a>, <a href="/wiki/Unix" title="Unix">Unix</a>, <a href="/wiki/OpenVMS" title="OpenVMS">OpenVMS</a> and <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">Windows</a>. In 1997, the <a href="/wiki/Gartner_Group" class="mw-redirect" title="Gartner Group">Gartner Group</a> reported that 80% of the world's business ran on COBOL with over 200 billion lines of code and 5 billion lines more being written annually.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[97]</a></sup></p>/n<p>Near the end of the 20th century, the <a href="/wiki/Year_2000_problem" title="Year 2000 problem">year 2000 problem</a> (Y2K) was the focus of significant COBOL programming effort, sometimes by the same programmers who had designed the systems decades before. The particular level of effort required to correct COBOL code has been attributed<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (March 2014)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> to the large amount of business-oriented COBOL, as business applications use dates heavily, and to fixed-length data fields. After the clean-up effort put into these programs for Y2K, a 2003 survey found that many remained in use.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarrKizior200316_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarrKizior200316-100">[98]</a></sup> The authors said that the survey data suggest "a gradual decline in the importance of Cobol in application development over the [following] 10 years unless ... integration with other languages and technologies can be adopted".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarrKizior200310_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarrKizior200310-101">[99]</a></sup></p>/n<p>In 2006 and 2012, <i><a href="/wiki/Computerworld" title="Computerworld">Computerworld</a></i> surveys found that over 60% of organizations used COBOL (more than <a href="/wiki/C%2B%2B" title="C++">C++</a> and <a href="/wiki/Visual_Basic_.NET" title="Visual Basic .NET">Visual Basic .NET</a>) and that for half of those, COBOL was used for the majority of their internal software.<sup id="cite_ref-Computerworld_Not_Dead_Yet_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Computerworld_Not_Dead_Yet-8">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[100]</a></sup> 36% of managers said they planned to migrate from COBOL, and 25% said they would like to if it was cheaper. Instead, some businesses have migrated their systems from expensive mainframes to cheaper, more modern systems, while maintaining their COBOL programs.<sup id="cite_ref-Computerworld_Not_Dead_Yet_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Computerworld_Not_Dead_Yet-8">[7]</a></sup></p>/n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Features">Features</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=COBOL&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Features">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Syntax">Syntax</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=COBOL&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Syntax">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<p>COBOL has an English-like syntax, which is used to describe nearly everything in a program. For example, a condition can be expressed as &#160;<code class="mw-highlight" id="" style="" dir="ltr"><span class="nv">x</span> <span class="kp">IS</span> <span class="ow">GREATER</span> <span class="ow">THAN</span> <span class="nv">y</span></code> or more concisely as &#160;<code class="mw-highlight" id="" style="" dir="ltr"><span class="nv">x</span> <span class="ow">GREATER</span> <span class="nv">y</span></code>&#160; or &#160;<code class="mw-highlight" id="" style="" dir="ltr"><span class="nv">x</span> <span class="o">&gt;</span> <span class="nv">y</span></code>. More complex conditions can be "abbreviated" by removing repeated conditions and variables. For example, &#160;<code class="mw-highlight" id="" style="" dir="ltr"><span class="nv">a</span> <span class="o">&gt;</span> <span class="nv">b</span> <span class="ow">AND</span> <span class="nv">a</span> <span class="o">&gt;</span> <span class="nv">c</span> <span class="ow">OR</span> <span class="nv">a</span> <span class="o">=</span> <span class="nv">d</span></code>&#160; can be shortened to <code class="mw-highlight" id="" style="" dir="ltr"><span class="nv">a</span> <span class="o">&gt;</span> <span class="nv">b</span> <span class="ow">AND</span> <span class="nv">c</span> <span class="ow">OR</span> <span class="o">=</span> <span class="nv">d</span></code>. As a consequence of this English-like syntax, COBOL has over 300 <a href="/wiki/Keyword_(computer_programming)" class="mw-redirect" title="Keyword (computer programming)">keywords</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEISO.2FIEC_JTC_1.2FSC_22.2FWG_42014.C2.A7_8.9_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEISO.2FIEC_JTC_1.2FSC_22.2FWG_42014.C2.A7_8.9-103">[101]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[c]</a></sup> Some of the keywords are simple alternative or pluralized spellings of the same word, which provides for more English-like statements and clauses; e.g., the <code class="mw-highlight" id="" style="" dir="ltr">IN</code> and <code class="mw-highlight" id="" style="" dir="ltr">OF</code> keywords can be used interchangeably, as can <code class="mw-highlight" id="" style="" dir="ltr">IS</code> and <code class="mw-highlight" id="" style="" dir="ltr">ARE</code>, and <code class="mw-highlight" id="" style="" dir="ltr">VALUE</code> and <code class="mw-highlight" id="" style="" dir="ltr">VALUES</code>.</p>/n<p>Each COBOL program is made up of four basic <a href="/wiki/Lexical_items" class="mw-redirect" title="Lexical items">lexical items</a>: words, literals, picture character-strings (see <a href="#PICTURE_clause">§&#160;PICTURE clause</a>) and separators. Words include reserved words and user-defined identifiers. They are up to 31 characters long and may include letters, digits, hyphens and underscores. Literals include numerals (e.g. <code class="mw-highlight" id="" style="" dir="ltr">12</code>) and strings (e.g. <code class="mw-highlight" id="" style="" dir="ltr">'Hello!'</code>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEISO.2FIEC_JTC_1.2FSC_22.2FWG_42014.C2.A7_8.3.1.2_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEISO.2FIEC_JTC_1.2FSC_22.2FWG_42014.C2.A7_8.3.1.2-106">[103]</a></sup> Separators include the space character and commas and semi-colons followed by a space.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEISO.2FIEC_JTC_1.2FSC_22.2FWG_42014.C2.A7_8.3.2_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEISO.2FIEC_JTC_1.2FSC_22.2FWG_42014.C2.A7_8.3.2-107">[104]</a></sup></p>/n<p>A COBOL program is split into four divisions: the identification division, the environment division, the data division and the procedure division. The identification division specifies the name and type of the source element and is where classes and interfaces are specified. The environment division specifies any program features that depend on the system running it, such as <a href="/wiki/Computer_files" class="mw-redirect" title="Computer files">files</a> and <a href="/wiki/Character_sets" class="mw-redirect" title="Character sets">character sets</a>. The data division is used to declare <a href="/wiki/Variable_(computer_science)" title="Variable (computer science)">variables</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parameter_(computer_programming)" title="Parameter (computer programming)">parameters</a>. The procedure division contains the program's <a href="/wiki/Statement_(computer_science)" title="Statement (computer science)">statements</a>. Each division is sub-divided into sections, which are made up of paragraphs.</p>/n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Metalanguage">Metalanguage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=COBOL&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Metalanguage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>/n<p>COBOL's syntax is usually described with a unique <a href="/wiki/Metalanguage" title="Metalanguage">metalanguage</a> using braces, brackets, bars and underlining. The metalanguage was developed for the original COBOL specifications. Although <a href="/wiki/Backus%E2%80%93Naur_form" title="Backus–Naur form">Backus–Naur form</a> did exist at the time, the committee had not heard of it.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShneiderman1985349_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShneiderman1985349-108">[105]</a></sup></p>/n<table class="wikitable">/n<caption>Elements of COBOL's metalanguage</caption>/n<tr>/n<th>Element</th>/n<th>Appearance</th>/n<th>Function</th>/n</tr>/n<tr>/n<td>All capitals</td>/n<td>EXAMPLE</td>/n<td>Reserved word</td>/n</tr>/n<tr>/n<td>Underlining</td>/n<td><span style="text-decoration: underline;">EXAMPLE</span></td>/n<td>The reserved word is compulsory</td>/n</tr>/n<tr>/n<td>Braces</td>/n<td>{ }</td>/n<td>Only one option may be selected</td>/n</tr>/n<tr>/n<td>Brackets</td>/n<td>[]</td>/n<td>Zero or one options may be selected</td>/n</tr>/n<tr>/n<td>Ellipsis</td>/n<td>...</td>/n<td>The preceding element may be repeated</td>/n</tr>/n<tr>/n<td rowspan="2">Bars</td>/n<td>{| |}</td>/n<td>One or more options may be selected. Any option may only be selected once.</td>/n</tr>/n<tr>/n<td>[| |]</td>/n<td>Zero or more options may be selected. Any option may only be selected once.</td>/n</tr>/n</table>/n<p>As an example, consider the following description of an <code>ADD</code> statement:</p>/n<p><span class="mwe-math-element"><span
